Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
Companion's Interpretation: Mujmal vs. Zahir in Hanafi Usul
Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Companion's ta'wil: mujmal vs zahir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the authority of a Companion's interpretation (ta'wil) of mujmal versus zahir texts, and apply the Hanafi rule to derive the ruling on the khiyar hadith.

Core Idea
Companion's interpretation binds only for clear texts (zahir), not for ambiguous ones (mujmal).

This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahamut' presents a nuanced Hanafi usul principle regarding the authority of a Companion's interpretation. The author contrasts the treatment of mujmal (ambiguous) texts with zahir (apparent) texts, using the famous hadith on the option of withdrawal in sales as an example. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi usul to hadith interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Define mujmal and zahir in Hanafi usul
Clarify the two textual categories: mujmal is ambiguous and not a proof by itself; zahir is clear and constitutes a proof. This distinction drives the difference in how Companion interpretations are treated.
Mujmal requires external clarification; zahir is self-evident unless overridden.
2
State the Hanafi position on Companion ta'wil
Hanafis accept a Companion's interpretation when he carries a zahir text away from its apparent meaning, because his uprightness guarantees he has evidence. They do not accept his interpretation of a mujmal text because the text itself is not evidence.
This is the core thesis of the passage.
3
Examine the khiyar hadith as an example
The hadith 'al-bayyi'ani bil-khiyar ma lam yatafarraqa' can be interpreted as option of the session (Shafi'i) or option of acceptance (Hanafi). Ibn 'Umar, the narrator, interpreted it as option of the session. Hanafis rejected his interpretation because the hadith is mujmal and not a proof until clarified.
The Qur'anic verse 4:29 requiring mutual consent supports the Hanafi interpretation.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i objection
Shafi'is argue that both cases involve following the Companion's opinion, so why accept in one and not the other? The Hanafi response is that in the mujmal case, the text is not a proof, so the Companion's opinion is just an opinion; in the zahir case, the text is a proof and abandoning it requires evidence which the Companion is presumed to have.
The key is the evidentiary status of the text itself.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Shafi'is rule that the option of withdrawal continues until the parties physically separate (option of the session). Hanafis rule that the option ends when the transaction is concluded by acceptance (option of acceptance), not requiring physical separation.
This fiqh difference stems directly from the usul dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جرى على اصطلاح الشافعية فإن المجمل عندهم غير متضح المعنى، وحينئذ لا يسلم عدم الحمل إلا بالقرينة المعاينة، بل يجوز حمله على أحد المعنيين بالرأي أبو بكونه مأنوساً بالنسبة إلى الآخر، رواية لا يكون حجة، ومن أوجب منا تقليد الصحابة فإنما يوجب لاحتمال السماع، وههنا قد ظهر أن لا سماع ، فلو كان حجة لزم تقليد المجتهد رأى الغير وهو يخطىء ويصيب، وأكثر مشايخنا لا يقبلون تأويل الصحابي، وتعيين أحد المحامل لما بينا، مثاله قوله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام : البيعان بالخيار ما لم يتفرقا يحتمل أن يكون المعنى هما بالخيار ما لم تتفرق أبدانهما فيدل على خيار المجلس كما يقول به الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى، ويحتمل أن يكون المعنى هما بالخيار ما داما متبايعين ما لم تتفرق أقوالهما فيدل على خيار القبول، وابن عمر الراوي حمله على الأول، ومشايخنا الكرام لم يقلدوه وحملوا على الثاني لما أن في إثبات إبطال حق الغير الذي تعلف به من غير رضا ذي الحق وإطلاق الله تعالى بقوله: ﴿إِلَّا أَن تَكُونَ تِجَرَةً عَن تَرَاضِ ﴾ [النساء : ۲۹] يقتضي جواز التصرف من غير توقف على خيار المجلس فافهم ولو) (حمل ذلك الصحابي الراوي (ظاهراً على غيره، كتخصيص العام، فالأكثر من الشافعية والمالكية يحملون على الظاهر، وفيه قال الشافعي : كيف أترك الحديث بقول من لو عاصرته لحاججته) أي كيف أترك القول الواجب الاتباع بقول من ليس قوله حجة (أقول : ما الفرق بين (الأول) وهو حمل المجمل على أحد المعنيين (والثاني) وهو حمل الظاهر على خلافه، فإن الحمل الأول أيضاً قول من لا حجية في قوله كالثاني فليس بين الصورتين فرق ولو قيل) في الأول ترجيح أحد المتساويين وفي الثاني ترجيح المرجوح وترجيح أحد المتساويين أهون من ترجيح (المرجوح) فيحتمل الأول دون الثاني، وتفصيله أن في الأول الخبر ليس حجة في نفسه لإجماله وإنما يحتمل الحجية بالبيان، والراوي قد بين، فيقبل بخلاف الثاني فإن الخبر حجة في نفسه، فحمله مبطل الحجية فلا يعتد به (لم يفد لأن كلا الحملين لا بد فيهما من قرينة فإن الحكم بتعيين المراد لا يتأتى من غير قرينة، فترجيح أحد المتساويين والمرجوح سيان في صيرورتهما حجة بالقرينة، فإن كان تأويله بالقرينة حجة فكلاهما حجة وإلا فلا شيء منهما حجة فما الفرق وفي بعض النسخ لم يبعد مكان لم يفد ولا يظهر له وجه والحنفية والحنابلة) يحملون ( على ما حمل) ذلك الصحابي الراوي لأن ترك الظاهر بلا موجب حرام وإذا هو عادل لا سيما إذا كان ممن أسلم قبل الفتح ودخل البيعة فلا يتركه إلا بدليل قطعاً وهذا الدليل إما السمع أو القرينة المعانية، وكلاهما موجبان أن المحمول عليه مراد الله ورسوله فيجب اتباعه بخلاف الصورة الأولى فإن المحتمل للمعاني يجوز مخالفة أحدها والعمل بالآخر بالرأي فقط، ولا ينافي العدالة فيتأتى من الصحابي، فلا قطع فيها بالحمل بالسماع أو القرينة المعاينة على أنه المراد، فلا يجب اتباعه، فاتضح الفرق واندفع ما يقال : ما بال الحنفية لا يقبلون حمل الصحابي في الأول دون الثاني مع أن في الثاني إبطال الحجة دون الأول فافهم. فإن قيل : يجوز ظن الصحابي غير القرينة قرينة، والخطأ في الحمل فلا يكون حجة قال (وأما تجويز خطئه بظن ما ليس دليلا على الصرف (دليلاً) عليه فمندفع بأن المراد الرجحان بالمعانية غالباً فافهم) يعني أن غالب حاله عمله بالسماع أو القرينة المعاينة لعدالته فيكون المحمول عليه مراد الله ورسوله، ولا ندعي القطع به، فإن الظن واجب العمل، ولا ينافيه هذا التجويز، بل نقول: هذا لتجويز غير ناشيء عن الدليل لا سيما في مثل الخلفاء والعبادلة فلا اعتداد به
According to the terminology of the Shafi'is, the mujmal is, in their view, unclear in meaning, and then the non-carriage is not accepted except with an evidentiary context, rather it is permissible to carry it upon one of the two meanings by opinion, because it is familiar relative to the other. A narration is not a proof. And whoever among us obliges taqlid of the Companions, he obliges it due to the possibility of hearing, and here it has appeared that there is no hearing. So if it were a proof, it would necessitate taqlid of a mujtahid following the opinion of another, and he errs and is correct. Most of our shaykhs do not accept the ta'wil of a Companion and the specification of one of the [possible] meanings due to what we have explained. An example of it is his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'The two transactors have the option of withdrawal as long as they have not separated.' It is possible that the meaning is: the two have the option as long as their bodies have not separated, indicating the option of the session, as al-Shafi'i says. And it is possible that the meaning is: the two have the option as long as they are transacting, as long as their words have not separated, indicating the option of acceptance. Ibn 'Umar, the narrator, carried it upon the first. Our noble shaykhs did not follow him and carried it upon the second because establishing the nullification of the right of another, which is attached to it, without the consent of the one possessing the right, and the absolute statement of Allah Most High: 'unless it be a trade by mutual consent' requires the permissibility of disposition without stopping on the option of the session, so understand. And if that Companion narrator carries an apparent [text] upon other than it, like specification of the general, then most of the Shafi'is and Malikis carry upon the apparent. And concerning it al-Shafi'i said: 'How can I abandon the hadith for the saying of someone with whom, if I had lived contemporaneously, I would have debated him?' i.e., how can I abandon the saying that is obligatory to follow for the saying of one whose saying is not a proof? I say: What is the difference between the first, which is carrying the mujmal upon one of the two meanings, and the second, which is carrying the apparent upon contrary to it? For the first carrying is also the saying of one whose saying has no authority like the second, so there is no difference between the two cases. And if it is said: In the first is the preferment of one of two equal [probabilities], and in the second is the preferment of the preponderated, and the preferment of one of two equals is easier than the preferment of the preponderated, so the first is possible without the second? Its detail is that in the first the report is not a proof in itself due to its ambiguity; rather, it may become a proof by clarification, and the narrator has clarified, so it is accepted. Contrary to the second, for the report is a proof in itself, so its carrying nullifies the proof, so it is not considered. It is not useful because both carriages inevitably require a context; indeed, the judgment of specifying the intended meaning cannot occur without a context. So the preferment of one of two equals and the preponderated are the same in both becoming proof by the context. If his interpretation by context is proof, then both are proof; otherwise, neither is proof, so what is the difference? In some copies: 'it is not far' in place of 'it is not useful', and its face does not appear. The Hanafis and Hanbalis carry upon what that Companion narrator carried because abandoning the apparent without cause is forbidden, and since he is upright, especially if he was among those who accepted Islam before the conquest and entered the allegiance, he does not abandon it except by definitive evidence. This evidence is either hearing or an evidentiary context seen, and both necessitate that what is carried upon is the intent of Allah and His Messenger, so it is obligatory to follow it. Contrary to the first case, for the one open to [multiple] meanings, it is permissible to violate one of them and act upon the other by opinion only, and it does not contradict uprightness, so it can occur from a Companion. Thus, there is no cut in the carriage by hearing or evidentiary context that it is the intended meaning, so it is not obligatory to follow it. So the difference becomes clear and what is said is repelled: 'Why do the Hanafis not accept the Companion's carriage in the first but [accept] in the second, even though in the second there is nullification of the proof without the first?' So understand. If it is said: It is possible for the Companion to consider something that is not a context as a context, and error in carriage, so it is not a proof. He said: 'And as for considering the possibility of his error in thinking what is not a proof as a proof, that is repelled because the intended is preponderance by observation most often, so understand.' That is, most of his state is acting upon hearing or the evidentiary context seen due to his uprightness, so what is carried upon is the intent of Allah and His Messenger, and we do not claim certainty about it, for probability necessitates action, and this possibility does not contradict it. Rather, we say: This possibility arises not from evidence, especially in the case of the Caliphs and the 'Abadilah, so it is not considered.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the authority of a Companion's interpretation. The author clarifies the Hanafi position: when a hadith is mujmal (ambiguous, admitting multiple meanings), the Companion's interpretation is not binding because the text itself is not a proof until clarified. However, when the text is zahir (apparent, clear in one meaning) and the Companion carries it to a different meaning (takhsis or otherwise), the Hanafis accept it, because the upright Companion would not abandon the apparent without a definitive evidence (hearing or observed context). The example of the hadith 'al-bayyi'ani bil-khiyar...' illustrates the point: Ibn 'Umar interpreted it as the option of the session (majlis), but the Hanafis preferred the option of acceptance (qabul) based on the Qur'anic verse on mutual consent. The author then responds to a Shafi'i objection that both cases involve following the Companion's opinion, explaining that in the case of mujmal the text is not evidence, so the Companion's clarification gives it evidentiary weight, while in the zahir case the text is already evidence and the Companion's departure from it requires a strong reason. The final part addresses the possibility that the Companion might mistake a non-context for a context, but argues that due to his uprightness, this possibility is remote and does not undermine the binding nature of his interpretation in the zahir case.

Key Terms
المجمل (al-mujmal): A text that is unclear and admits multiple possible meanings, not constituting a proof by itself.
الظاهر (az-zahir): A text whose meaning is apparent and clear, constituting a proof that cannot be abandoned without a strong reason.
تأويل الصحابي (ta'wil as-sahabi): The interpretation of a Companion regarding the meaning of a text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis accept a Companion's interpretation when he carries a zahir text to a different meaning, but not when he interprets a mujmal text?
A) Because the Companion is always correct in interpreting mujmal texts.
B) Because in the case of mujmal, the text itself is not a proof, so the Companion's opinion is not binding; in zahir, the text is a proof and the Companion's departure from it requires evidence which his uprightness ensures.
C) Because Hanafis do not value the opinions of Companions at all.
D) Because the Shafi'is forced them to accept in the second case.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a Companion's interpretation is authoritative when it departs from a clear (zahir) text, but not when it merely clarifies an ambiguous (mujmal) text.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
12 min
The Permissibility of Narrating Hadith by Meaning: A Hanafi Usul Dispute
Dispute over narrating hadith by meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position on narrating hadith by meaning and rebut the opposing arguments using the dalil presented.

Core Idea
Narrating hadith by meaning is permissible if the narrator preserves the legal intent and does not change lawful/unlawful.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut presents a classical usul dispute on whether a hadith may be transmitted in paraphrased form. The Hanafi school, represented by Imam Fakhr al-Islam al-Pazdawi, permits it under strict conditions. Opponents raise objections based on literal transmission commands, risk of distortion, and fear of taqlid of the narrator. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to evaluate hadith transmission and the use of dalil in usul.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the Hanafi position
Identify that the issue is al-riwaya bi al-ma'na (narrating by meaning) and the Hanafi position is permissibility with conditions: no change in lawful/unlawful and meaning must be clear.
The Hanafi position is permissive, not absolute; it requires the narrator to be reliable.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proofs
Examine the dalil: the hadith of Ya'qub (permission to convey meaning), analogy to translating the Quran for non-Arabs, and the argument that the purpose of hadith is the meaning (ma'na) not the wording.
The Hanafis also rebut the objection of decline (inhitat) by stating the obligation is to convey rulings, not eloquence.
3
Examine the opponents' arguments
Opponents present three proofs: the hadith 'Naddara Allahu imra'an' commanding exact transmission, the risk of gradual obliteration of hadith, and that narrating by meaning leads to taqlid of the narrator.
The opponents rely on literal interpretation of the command 'as he heard it'.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi responses
For each opponent argument, see the Hanafi rebuttal: the hadith is about conveying meaning (fiqh), obliteration is avoided if no change occurs, and ijtihad is on the meaning intended by the Prophet, not the narrator's wording.
The Hanafis also note that the hadith itself is transmitted with different wordings, showing that some degree of meaning transmission is unavoidable.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical difference is whether a hadith can be paraphrased for understanding. The author supports the Hanafi position, which is mufta bihi.
The dispute is about method, not the permissibility of all paraphrasing; both sides agree that distortion is forbidden.

مراداً له عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام فيجتهد المجتهد في هذا اللفظ، وهذا المعنى، فيلزم تقليد الراوي، وحينئذ اندفع الجواب، ووجهه بأن بقاء معناه الواقعي غير لازم، والمعنى المفهوم باقٍ، ثم أجاب ونقله فلا تدليس، وإنما التدليس إذا علم أن له محملاً آخر ونقل ما حمله عليه وأما قوله إنه وقع الاتفاق عليه، من موارد النزاع المشترك أيضاً انتهى كلامه الشريف وأنت إذا تأملت فيما بينا في تقرير كلام الإمام ففيه أن
(مسألة: حذف البعض من الخبر (ورواية البعض منه (جائز إن لم يكن بينهما)
(Their intent, i.e., the intent of the narrators, is that in every case) from his saying: 'exactly it, or like it, or close to it' (is narration) of the hadith of the Messenger of God (peace be upon him and his family and companions). So it is against you, O preventers, not for you. So reflect upon it and do not pay attention to what is said: 'How do you know that such was their intent?' Rather, it is possible that mentioning 'like it' is a caution against narrating by meaning and an indication that it is not the exact saying of the Messenger, as is not hidden from one who has the slightest understanding of purposes and conversations. And it may be argued by what al-Khatib narrated from Ya'qub ibn 'Abd Allah ibn Sulayman al-Laythi from his father from his grandfather: 'We came to the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) and said: By our fathers and mothers, we hear from you but cannot convey it as we heard it from you. He said: If you do not make lawful what is unlawful and do not make unlawful what is lawful, and you hit the meaning, then there is no harm.' And it is also narrated as mursal to 'Abd Allah ibn Sulayman. The Imam of the people of the path and the Shari'a, al-Hasan al-Basri, when mentioned to him, said: 'Were it not for this, we would not have narrated.' And what is said that it is mursal, for 'Abd Allah ibn Sulayman is a Tabi'i in the Sahih, so it is harmless because the mursal is a proof, especially when supported by the practice of most scholars. Know that these arguments do not negate the opinion of Imam Fakhr al-Islam, because it is possible that [the hadith] is among those whose meaning is clear; rather, the last proof supports it, for permission is only when it is known that the forbidden is not made lawful and the lawful not made forbidden, and that is when the meaning is clear, as is not hidden from one of sagacity. So understand. And first, they argued by the permissibility of interpreting it in a non-Arabic language by consensus, and that is narrating by meaning. And it was answered that it (interpretation in a non-Arabic language) is expression for non-Arabs, meaning that permission there is only as an interpretation for them to understand. It is not permissible there to attribute it to the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) by saying 'this is his saying.' So what is permissible there is not narrating by meaning, and what is narrating by meaning is not permissible there, and the permission due to necessity of their understanding does not necessitate permission where there is no necessity. (And) they argued: Secondly, the purpose in the hadith is only the meaning, not the wording, because it is revelation not recited, so the wording is not observed, and that is achieved in narrating by meaning, so it is permissible. (It is said: We do not accept that it is only the purpose; rather, seeking blessing in his wording (peace be upon him and his family) is also [a purpose], so observing it may be obligatory for this. And the answer: The most important purpose is related to knowledge of divine rulings, and the ruling of the hadith is not attached to its wording, so it is permissible to convey its meaning such that the intended ruling is derived from it, and that suffices for the purpose. As for seeking blessing, even if it is also a purpose, it only indicates recommendation, and it is a resolve but not obligatory. And if it is accepted that the purpose is meaning, we do not accept that it is a complete cause for permissibility of narrating by meaning, such that it necessitates its permissibility. The preventing factor is (decline), i.e., the decline of the speech of the most eloquent of human beings to the speech of common individuals. And its answer: The obligation is to convey the legal rulings so that the benefit of the mission is not lost. In the Sunnah, rulings are derived from meaning; the ruling is not dependent on wording, nor is observance of eloquence required, for there is no inimitability in it. So the purpose of conveying the laws is achieved by conveying the meaning of the hadith. As for the Book, because it is miraculous and attached to legal rulings, it is necessary to convey its wording as well. So understand.
The preventers said: First, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'May God brighten the face of a person who hears my hadith...' meaning: 'May God brighten the face of a person who hears my speech, understands it, preserves it, and conveys it. For many a carrier of jurisprudence is not a jurist...' as he heard. It comes from Fawatih al-Rahmut sharh Muslim al-Thubut, part two. And in another narration: 'May God brighten the face of a person who hears my speech, understands it, and conveys it as he heard it. For many a conveyer is more understanding than the listener, and many a carrier of jurisprudence is not a jurist, and many a carrier of jurisprudence conveys it to one who is more jurisprudent than him.' Narrated by the authors of the Sunan. (We said: The conveyer) by meaning (conveys as he heard), for what is meant by conveying it is conveying its meaning, like the translator into a non-Arabic language, for he is a conveyer. And it is indicated by his saying: 'and many a carrier of jurisprudence,' for jurisprudence is related to meaning, not wording. (And if it is accepted) that the conveyer by meaning is not a conveyer as he heard, then it is a supplication for him (i.e., for the conveyer of the exact wording) because it is primary and a resolve, and it does not necessitate the impermissibility of narrating by meaning, nor does it contradict 'many a carrier of jurisprudence is not a jurist' etc., because the meaning: many a carrier of jurisprudence is not a jurist, so it is possible that narrating by meaning leads to confusion of meaning, and with possibility nothing becomes obligatory; its utmost is recommendation. Moreover, in this argument there is a corruption of formulation, for the hadith is narrated with different wordings, so it is not free from narrating by meaning. So if it were not valid to act upon it. (And) they said: Secondly, if narrating by meaning were permissible, it would gradually lead to obliteration of the hadith, for if the first narrated it by meaning the hadith would change, then after narrating it likewise in another degree, further change would occur, then and so on until the meaning is obliterated. (We said: The discussion is on the assumption of no change at all, and then there is no obliteration. But if it changes in any way, then it is not permissible and not accepted; rather, this is not the act of a just jurist at all.) (And) they said: Thirdly, as was said in the glosses of Mirzajan: 'If narrating by meaning were valid, it would necessitate taqlid of the narrator,' which is invalid because the mujtahid only exercised ijtihad on his (the narrator's) wording and derived the ruling from it, not on the noble wording (of the Prophet) at that time, i.e., when it is narrated by meaning. (I say: If the meaning of the Prophet (peace be upon him) remains as is apparent to the knowledgeable jurist) conveyer, then the wording is subordinate, and ijtihad occurs only on the meaning, which is from the Messenger of God, so taqlid of the narrator is not necessitated. (Otherwise, i.e., if the meaning does not remain, then there is no dispute) about its impermissibility. (Moreover, it does not prevent narrating with a synonym) for it conveys the same meaning, so ijtihad on it is ijtihad on the meaning intended by him (peace be upon him), so there is no taqlid of the narrator. Mutli' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya (may God sanctify his spirit) said: There is a kind of distortion in it, for the author of the glosses completed with this aspect the second argument, not that he brought another aspect. He stated that it is permissible to understand from the hadith and narrate it with another wording, while it may not be intended by him (peace be upon him), so the mujtahid exercises ijtihad on this wording and this meaning, thus necessitating taqlid of the narrator. Then he answered that the just skilled narrator does not narrate according to his understanding due to the possibility that it is not intended, otherwise it would be deception. It is not unlikely that in this image agreement occurred on impermissibility. Mutli' al-Asrar (may God sanctify his spirit) critiqued: When he thinks that it is intended by the Lawgiver and narrates it, there is no deception; deception only occurs when he knows that it has another interpretation and narrates what he interprets. As for his saying that agreement occurred on it, it is also from the common grounds of dispute. His noble speech ended. And if you reflect on what we have clarified in the exposition of the speech of Imam Fakhr al-Islam (may God sanctify his spirit), you will know that this is repelled in the most complete ways. So contemplate.

So in it that
(Issue: Deleting part of the report and narrating a part of it is permissible if there is no between them)

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding the permissibility of narrating hadith by meaning (al-riwaya bi al-ma'na) rather than verbatim. The author presents arguments from both the proponents (al-mujizun) and opponents (al-mani'un), following the structure typical in Hanafi usul works. The proponents first argue that the intent of narrators is to convey the meaning, using evidence from the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) who allowed hitting the meaning as long as lawful/unlawful are not changed, and from the hadith of the Prophet narrated by al-Hasan al-Basri. They also argue by analogy to the permissibility of translating the Quran for non-Arabs (which is a form of narrating by meaning) and by the fact that the purpose of hadith is the meaning (ma'na) not the wording (lafz), as it is non-recited revelation (ghayr matlu). They also respond to the objection of decline (inhitat) by stating that the obligation is to convey rulings, not to preserve eloquence. The opponents present three arguments: first, the hadith 'Naddara Allahu imra'an' which commands conveying exactly as heard; second, the risk of gradual obliteration of the hadith; third, that it would lead to taqlid of the narrator. The Hanafi position, as defended by Imam Fakhr al-Islam (likely al-Pazdawi), rebuts each: the hadith is about conveying the meaning (as explained by 'carrier of fiqh'), the risk of obliteration only applies if change occurs (which is not allowed anyway), and the concern of taqlid is unfounded because ijtihad is on the meaning, not the narrator's wording. A subtle point from the glosses of Mirzajan and the response from Mutli' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya further refines the dispute. The passage ends by introducing the next issue: narrating only part of a hadith.

Key Terms
الرواية بالمعنى (al-riwaya bi al-ma'na): Transmitting the meaning of a hadith rather than its exact wording.
الانحطاط (al-inhitat): The decline or lowering of the Prophet's eloquent speech to the level of common speech when paraphrased.
تقليد الراوي (taqlid al-rawi): Blindly following the narrator's understanding rather than the Prophet's intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, what is a key condition for the permissibility of narrating a hadith by meaning?
A) The narrator must be a mujtahid.
B) The meaning must be clear and no change in lawful/unlawful occurs.
C) The exact wording must be preserved as well.
D) The hadith must be from mutawatir narrations.

Key Takeaway
Narrating hadith by meaning is permissible in Hanafi usul when the narrator preserves the legal intent and does not alter the lawful/unlawful, allowing flexibility without compromising the Sunnah.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
6 min
The Hanafi Ruling on 'Qala Rajul'
Ruling on 'qala rajul' with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for accepting or rejecting a narration introduced by 'qala rajul' and distinguish it from mursal.

Core Idea
A narration from an unknown narrator is rejected; specifying trustworthiness or Companionship elevates it.

This lesson examines the status of the phrase 'a man said' in hadith transmission according to the Hanafi school. The passage clarifies that such a narration is generally not accepted due to the unknown narrator, but it distinguishes this from mursal and provides exceptions when the narrator is specified as trustworthy or a Companion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on 'qala rajul'
State the default position: it is not accepted.
2
Distinguish from mursal
Explain why 'qala rajul' differs from mursal: mursal implies direct attribution to a known source, while 'qala rajul' is anonymous.
3
Identify exceptions
Recognise that if the narrator says 'a trustworthy man' or 'a man from the Companions', the report is accepted.
Companions are all upright.
4
Consider conventional usage
If 'a man' refers to a specific known trustworthy individual by convention, acceptance is allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فرع): قال رجل : لا يقبل في المذهب (الصحيح) وليس هذا كالارسال كما نقل عن شمس الأئمة، لأن هذا رواية عن مجهول والإرسال جزم بنسبة المتن : إلى الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم وهذا لا يكون إلا بالتوثيق فافترقا (بخلاف) قال (ثقة) أو رجل من الصحابة، لأن هذا رواية عن ثقة لأن الصحابة كلهم عدول (ولو) اصطلح على معين معلوم العدالة على التعيين برجل (فلا إشكال في القبول .
(Branch): A man said: It is not accepted in the (correct) school, and this is not like mursal, as transmitted from Shams al-A'imma, because this is a narration from an unknown person, while mursal is a definitive attribution of the text to the Messenger ﷺ and his Companions, and this is only done with authentication, so they differ. (Contrary to) if he said: (a trustworthy man) or a man from the Companions, because this is a narration from a trustworthy person, since all Companions are upright. (And if) a specific person of known uprightness is referred to by the word 'a man', then there is no doubt in its acceptance.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a branch ruling (far') concerning how to treat narrations introduced with the phrase 'a man said' (qala rajul). The correct position in the Hanafi school, as transmitted from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, is that such a statement is not accepted as a valid report. This is because it is a narration from an unknown person (majhul). The passage distinguishes this from a mursal hadith, where the narrator explicitly attributes the text to the Prophet or Companions, which requires documentation (tawthiq) and thus has stronger evidentiary value. However, if the narrator specifies that the report is from a 'trustworthy man' (thiqah) or from a Companion (since all Companions are considered upright), then it is accepted. Similarly, if the phrase 'a man' is a convention (istilah) for a specific known trustworthy person, there is no issue in accepting it. Thus, the key is the ambiguity versus specificity of the narrator's identity.

Key Terms
قال رجل (qāla rajul): A statement indicating a narration from an unspecified person
الإرسال (al-irsāl): Attributing a hadith to the Prophet or Companion without mentioning the intermediary

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the branch ruling, when is the phrase 'a man said' accepted?
A) It is always accepted because it is like mursal.
B) It is accepted if the narrator specifies 'a trustworthy man' or a Companion.
C) It is accepted only if the report is about a legal ruling.
D) It is never accepted under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of a hadith hinges on the identification of the narrator; ambiguity leads to rejection in the Hanafi school.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Conflict between Khabar and Qiyas: Hanafi Preference for the Report
Usul dispute on the preference between khabar and qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on preferring khabar over qiyas and evaluate the evidence from 'Umar's practice as ijma.

Core Idea
When khabar and qiyas conflict, the Hanafi school prefers the khabar absolutely, proven by the consensus of the Companions.

This passage addresses a fundamental usul question: when a Prophetic report and analogical reasoning point to different rulings, which takes precedence? The text surveys multiple opinions, from the absolute preference for the report held by the majority (including Abu Hanifa) to the absolute preference for qiyas attributed to Malik, and then presents the Hanafi proof from the actions of 'Umar. You will navigate this dispute and understand why the Hanafi school champions the report over analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue and the consensus
The text states that when khabar and qiyas conflict and cannot be reconciled, there is consensus that the more preponderant zann is to be followed. The disagreement is over which is inherently more preponderant.
Zann here refers to probable knowledge, not certainty.
2
List the major positions
The majority (Hanafi, Shafi'i, Hanbali) hold that the report is absolutely preponderant. Imam Malik holds qiyas is absolutely preponderant. Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili conditions on the 'illa being definitive. Isa ibn Aban conditions on the narrator's precision. Imam Fakhr al-Islam conditions on the narrator's level of ijtihad. Later scholars (al-mukhtar) distinguish cases based on strength of 'illa.
Each position has a different threshold for what constitutes preponderance.
3
Focus on the Hanafi proof: 'Umar's practice as ijma
The Hanafi position is supported by three incidents from Caliph 'Umar: the fetus diya (ghurra), the diya of fingers, and the wife's inheritance from her husband's diya. In each case, 'Umar abandoned qiyas upon hearing a hadith. The Companions did not object, establishing silent ijma (consensus) on preferring the report.
This is a form of ijma suquti (silent consensus).
4
Understand the rebuttal to the objection
A possible objection: the evidence only shows permissibility of acting by the report, not obligation of precedence. The author responds that the silence of the Companions indicates approval and thus obligation, as they would have spoken if they disagreed.
This follows the usul principle that silence in the face of a public practice constitutes tacit approval.
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
In a conflict, a Hanafi mufti first checks if the khabar is clear in indication (zahir al-dalala) and the narrator is trustworthy. If so, it overrides any qiyas. Only in rare cases (e.g., narrator not precise) might qiyas be considered.
This application is derived from the views of Isa ibn Aban and Fakhr al-Islam as clarifications of the three imams' position.

Allah Almighty. Issue: When a report (khabar) and analogy (qiyas) conflict, and it is impossible to reconcile them, as when both are specific (khass) and no indication of figurativeness, or both are general and equal, etc., then there is consensus on preferring the more preponderant of the two kinds of probability (zann). (But) the disagreement is over which probability is preponderant. The majority of usul scholars – including the three imams: Abu Hanifa, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad (may Allah be pleased with them), and the two companions Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, indeed most of the followers of the Great Imam (may Allah have mercy on them) – said that this preponderance lies in the report absolutely. Perhaps what is meant by report is that it has clear indication (zahir al-dalala), not like the ambiguous (mushkil) or summary (mujmal). (And it is said) that preponderance lies in qiyas absolutely, which is attributed to Imam Malik. And Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili said: Qiyas is preferred if the establishment of the ratio legis ('illa) is definitive. If it is not definitive except for the original (asl) without the 'illa and its presence in the branch, then ijtihad is used in weighing; meaning neither is conclusively preponderant, but sometimes the report may be preponderant and sometimes qiyas, and this is known by ijtihad. (Otherwise) i.e. if neither the 'illa nor the original is definitively established, then the report is preponderant. And Isa ibn Aban (from us) said: If the narrator is precise (dabit) and not lenient, then the report is preponderant; otherwise it is a place for ijtihad. The conclusion is that the report is absolutely preponderant except when there is some leniency in the narrator. Then we look: if this leniency does not harm the soundness or goodness of the report, then the report; otherwise qiyas. This is in reality an explanation of the meaning from the three imams, not another school. And Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: If the narrator is among the mujtahids – like the four (Rightly Guided Caliphs) and the 'Abadila (in the margin: the 'Abadila: Ibn Abbas, Ibn Umar, Ibn al-Zubayr, and Ibn Amr ibn al-As; Ibn Mas'ud is not among them; al-Jawhari erred as in the Qamus. I say: This is according to the hadith scholars, but according to the Hanafi jurists, Ibn Mas'ud is among them, so the accusation of error is itself wrong.) and others like the Mother of the Believers Aisha al-Siddiqa, Mu'adh ibn Jabal, Zayd ibn Thabit, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Abu al-Darda', etc. (may Allah be pleased with them all) – then the report is preferred even if the narrator is known for uprightness ('adala) without jurisprudence (fiqh), like Abu Hurayra (and it has preceded that Abu Hurayra is undoubtedly a mujtahid faqih) and Anas ibn Malik. So the report is not abandoned due to opposition with qiyas except when the door of opinion (ra'y) is blocked, like the hadith of al-Musarra. The speech of Imam Fakhr al-Islam has passed in confirmation and establishment, and the summary of his view is that the report is absolutely preferred, but when the door of opinion is blocked, doubt arises in the matching (of the report to reality) when the narrator is not a faqih, so it is not accepted due to this doubt. This too is in reality in agreement with what is from the three imams. And al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani al-Shafi'i hesitated. The chosen view (al-mukhtar) among some later scholars: if the establishment of the 'illa is more preponderant than the report and its presence in the branch is not weakened (but remains at the level it had in the original or is preponderant in it), then qiyas is preferred. If they are equal (i.e., the establishment of the 'illa in the original and branch, and the establishment of the report), then suspension (tawaqquf), and neither is preponderant over the other. Otherwise (i.e., if neither is preponderant nor equal), then the report is preferred. (Our evidence) according to the chosen view of the later scholars: giving preponderance to the preponderant is a necessary obligation. So qiyas is preferred when the 'illa's establishment is preponderant, and the report is preferred when it is preponderant. And giving preponderance in case of equality is arbitrary; thus one must suspend definitively. Know that this is very obvious, and the three imams do not deny it, for preponderating the preponderant is necessary and agreed upon. Rather, the point of disagreement is whether such qiyas exists or not. Their (the early imams') focus is that it does not exist. So whoever claims that qiyas is preponderant even in one instance must prove it, and that is extremely difficult. And for the majority, first: the Commander of the Faithful 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) abandoned analogy in the case of the fetus, which was non-obligation of a diya (blood money) for the fetus like other doubtful matters, based on the report of Hamal ibn Malik that the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him and his Companions) obligated a ghurra (a slave) as reported. He said: 'Had I not heard this, we would have ruled by our opinion.' This was narrated by Abu Dawud. In another narration: 'It is a lie that we rule in this by our opinion.' And 'Umar abandoned analogy regarding the diya of fingers; his opinion was: for the little finger six (camels), for the ring finger nine, for the middle finger ten, for the index finger twelve (and in al-Tahrir: ten), for the thumb fifteen camels – all in at-Taysir. The commentator said: This is mentioned by many, but in the narration of al-Bayhaqi, he held for the index finger twelve, for the middle finger thirteen. Al-Shafi'i narrated his ruling on the thumb similarly based on the report of 'Amr ibn Hazm that for each finger ten camels in the book. And 'Umar abandoned analogy regarding the wife's inheritance from her husband's diya – the analogy indicating no inheritance since the husband did not possess her to inherit, and the marital relationship does not persist after death to establish inheritance by kinship – (and many other such widely spread incidents). No one from the Salaf or Khalaf denied this, so it is consensus on preferring the report. This is very clear to anyone who studies the events and histories. (I say: If it is said: The necessary conclusion from what you mentioned is permission to act by the report, not obligation of precedence. We say: Their silence in...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a major discussion (mas'ala) on the conflict between khabar (a hadith report) and qiyas (analogy). The author states that when the two conflict and cannot be reconciled, there is consensus that the more preponderant (arjah) of the two probabilities (zann) is to be followed. The disagreement is over which is inherently more preponderant. The majority of usul scholars, including Abu Hanifa, Shafi'i, Ahmad, and the two companions Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, hold that the report is absolutely preponderant, provided the report is clear in its indication (zahir al-dalala) and not ambiguous (mushkil) or summary (mujmal). Imam Malik is attributed the view that qiyas is absolutely preponderant. Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili distinguishes: if the 'illa (ratio legis) is established definitively, qiyas is preferred; otherwise, it depends on ijtihad. Isa ibn Aban conditions: if the narrator is precise (dabit) and not lenient (mutasahil), the report is preponderant; if lenient, the case is open to ijtihad. The actual position of the three imams, as clarified by Isa ibn Aban, is that the report is always preponderant except when the narrator has a type of leniency that damages the report's soundness; then qiyas may be used. Imam Fakhr al-Islam adds details about the narrator's level: if the narrator is from the mujtahids (like the four caliphs and the 'Abadila etc.), the report is preponderant even if the narrator is not a faqih; but if the narrator is merely upright without fiqh, the report is not abandoned for qiyas unless the door of opinion (ra'y) is blocked, as in the hadith of al-Musarra (a case of milking a she-camel). The text then presents the chosen view of some later scholars (al-mukhtar): if the establishment of the 'illa is more preponderant than the report and its presence in the branch is not weakened, qiyas is preferred; if equal, suspend; otherwise, the report is preferred. The author argues that this chosen view is actually in line with the three imams, since preponderating the preponderant is necessary. The real disagreement is whether such a qiyas that is preponderant exists; the three imams deny its existence. The author then provides historical evidence from the actions of Caliph 'Umar (RA): he abandoned qiyas in three cases (fetus diya, finger diya, wife's inheritance from husband's diya) due to a report, and these actions were not denied by anyone, indicating consensus (ijma') that the report is preferred over qiyas. The passage ends with a response to a potential objection that the evidence only shows permissibility (jawaz) of acting by the report, not obligation (wujub) of preferring it; the author says 'Their silence...' (incomplete).

Key Terms
تعارض الخبر والقياس (ta'āruḍ al-khabar wa al-qiyās): Conflict between a Prophetic report and analogical reasoning
الظن (al-ẓann): Probable knowledge derived from evidence; the basis for legal ruling in the absence of certainty
الإجماع السكوتي (al-ijmāʿ al-sukūtī): Silent consensus: where some Companions practice a ruling and others remain silent, indicating approval

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi usul scholars, which is preferred when a clear khabar conflicts with qiyas?
A) Qiyas is always preferred.
B) The report is absolutely preferred.
C) It depends on the strength of the 'illa.
D) The later scholars suspend judgment.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a clear Prophetic report (khabar) takes precedence over analogical reasoning (qiyas) in every case, as established by the practice of 'Umar and the silence of the Companions.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
Taqrir and Istibshar: Evaluating the Prophetic Silence in the Qiyafah Dispute
Hanafi refutation of Shafi'i use of taqrir to validate qiyafah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi critique of the Shafi'i argument that the Prophet's taqrir and istibshar establish qiyafah as a valid proof for lineage.

Core Idea
Silence and joy do not always imply approval—context and audience determine legal weight.

In this passage, the author presents a classic usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over whether the Prophet's tacit approval (taqrir) and joy (istibshar) can establish qiyafah (physiognomy) as a valid method for proving lineage. The Shafi'i school relies on an incident where the Prophet did not object to al-Mudlaji's statement about the lineage of Zayd and Usama, and even showed joy. The Hanafis refute this, arguing that the silence was due to the futility of objecting to hypocrites and that the believers already knew the truth. The lesson will guide you through the reasoning behind the Hanafi position and its implications for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i claim
Pinpoint the specific evidence (taqrir and istibshar) and the ruling derived by the Shafi'i school from the incident of al-Mudlaji.
The Shafi'i claim is that qiyafah is a valid method for establishing lineage.
2
State the Hanafi objection
Restate the Hanafi refutation: the silence was not an endorsement because the audience were hypocrites who would not benefit from objection.
The Hanafi position is that qiyafah is not a valid proof for lineage.
3
Analyze the taqrir argument
Examine the Hanafi response regarding the silence: it was due to futility, not approval. Contrast with the general usul principle that taqrir indicates permissibility only when the audience is receptive.
Key usul principle: silence under certain conditions does not indicate approval.
4
Analyze the istibshar argument
Understand that the Prophet's joy was not an endorsement of qiyafah but a means to silence the hypocrites who already accepted qiyafah as valid.
Istibshar is context-dependent; here it was a tactical response.
5
Evaluate the counter-analogy
The author gives an analogy of a disbeliever entering a church: the king does not forbid it because it would be futile, not because he approves. Apply this to the qiyafah case.
The analogy clarifies that taqrir does not necessarily imply approval.
6
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference: in the Hanafi school, lineage cannot be proven by qiyafah; in the Shafi'i school, it can.
This dispute has real consequences in family law cases.

Source Text

فرع: اعتبر الشافعي القيافة في إثبات النسب خلافاً للحنفية وتمسك) الشافعي (بترك الإنكار والاستبشار في قول المدلجي بضم الميم وسكون الدال رجل من بني مدلج وكان مشتهراً بمعرفة القيافة وقد بدت له أقدام زيد وأسامة حين كانا نائمين (هذه الأقدام بعضها من بعض فاستبشر بقوله فدل التقرير والاستبشار على أن القيافة مثبت للنسب (وأورد أن الإنكار لموافقة الحق) قوله : فلا يمكن رد قوله (والاستبشار) لم يكن لكون القيافة دليلاً على النسب، بل الحصول إلزام الطاعنين الذين هم المنافقون بحسب زعمهم الباطل فإنهم كانوا يرون القيافة حجة كاملة والمدلجي كان كاملاً في فن القيافة وكان منشأ طعنهم الاختلاف في لونيهما، فإن قلت فلم لم ينكر الطريق، ولو كان منكراً لأنكره ، قال (وأما ترك إنكار الطريق فلأنه كتردد كافر إلى (كنيسة وترك الإنكار عليه في هذا التردد لا يوجب الجواز قطعاً وذلك لأن الطاعنين هم (المنافقون فلا ينفعهم الإنكار فلذا لم يستغل به (وأما المؤمنون فقد ثبت عندهم انحصار النسب في الفراش وكانوا عالمين أن القيافة ليس بشيء، وأما خصوص نسب أسامة فعندهم كالشمس على نصف النهار لإخباره صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (فتدبر) .
Branch: Al-Shafi'i considered qiyafah (physiognomy) as proof for establishing lineage, contrary to the Hanafis. Al-Shafi'i adhered to the lack of objection and the joy in the statement of al-Mudlaji (with damm on mim and sukun on dal, a man from Banu Mudlaj who was famous for knowledge of qiyafah), who saw the feet of Zayd and Usama when they were sleeping, and some of these feet were from some, so he showed joy in his statement. So the tacit approval and the joy indicated that qiyafah establishes lineage. It is objected that the lack of objection was in accordance with the truth, so his statement cannot be refuted. And the joy was not because qiyafah is evidence for lineage, but rather it was to compel the critics, who were the hypocrites according to their false claim. For they considered qiyafah a complete proof, and al-Mudlaji was complete in the art of qiyafah, and the origin of their criticism was the difference in the two's colors. If you say: Why did he not forbid the practice? If it were forbidden, he would have forbidden it. He said: The reason for not forbidding is like a disbeliever's frequenting a church; not forbidding him does not necessitate permissibility absolutely. This is because the critics were the hypocrites, so objection would not benefit them, hence he did not stop them. As for the believers, the exclusivity of lineage to the marital bed was established among them, and they knew that qiyafah is nothing. As for the specific lineage of Usama, it was to them like the sun at midday due to his informing, peace be upon him and his family and companions. (Reflect).
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a branch (far') addressing a specific dispute between the Shafi'i and Hanafi schools regarding the validity of qiyafah (physiognomy) for proving lineage. The Shafi'i school argues that the Prophet's tacit approval (taqrir) and his joy (istibshar) in the statement of al-Mudlaji, who identified Zayd and Usama's lineage through their feet, indicate that qiyafah is a valid method. The Hanafi refutation provides several points: First, the Prophet's silence was not an endorsement of qiyafah but rather because the critics were hypocrites, who would not benefit from objection. Second, the believers among the companions already knew that lineage is established only through the marital bed (farash), not qiyafah. Third, the specific lineage of Usama was already known through explicit prophetic statement. The analogy of a disbeliever entering a church is given: the king does not forbid it because it would be futile, not because he approves of it. Thus, taqrir and istibshar in this context do not prove the validity of qiyafah.

Key Terms
القيافة (al-qiyafah): The practice of tracing lineage through physical features (physiognomy).
الاستبشار (al-istibshar): Showing joy or pleasure, often taken as a stronger indication of approval than mere silence.
التقرير (al-taqrir): Tacit approval; the Prophet's silence or absence of objection to an action, which is considered a source of law in usul.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Hanafis reject the Shafi'i use of taqrir and istibshar to prove qiyafah?
A) Because the Prophet explicitly forbade qiyafah
B) Because the hypocrites were not to be silenced
C) Because the silence was due to the hypocrites' stubbornness and the believers already knew the truth
D) Because al-Mudliji was not a reliable expert

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's taqrir and istibshar regarding al-Mudlaji's statement do not prove qiyafah's validity because the silence was due to futility and the joy was to silence hypocrites; context is essential in evaluating prophetic actions.

Was the Prophet Bound by Previous Sharia? The Hanafi View
Presenting the debate on whether the Prophet followed prior sharia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi preferred view on whether the Prophet was bound by previous sharia, and evaluate the key evidences and opposing positions.

Core Idea
The Prophet was bound by a previous sharia as Allah's ruling, not as a follower, likely that of Ibrahim.

One of the foundational usul questions is whether the Prophet Muhammad (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) was subject to the sharia of earlier prophets before his own prophethood. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position — that he was bound by a previous sharia as a ruling of Allah, not as a follower — and contrasts it with the views of other schools. Understanding this dispute is crucial for evaluating the evidentiary value of his pre-prophethood actions and for appreciating the continuity of divine guidance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the preferred view
State the central question: Was the Prophet bound by a previous sharia before his mission? The text states that al-mukhtar (the chosen view) is that he was bound by some previous sharia.
Note that 'al-mukhtar' is the Hanafi preferred position.
2
Understand the key distinction
Recognize that the binding is not as a follower of that prophet, but as a ruling of Allah. The text emphasizes that the Prophet followed it as hukm Allah, not as hukm dhalik al-nabi.
This distinction answers objections about following a human messenger.
3
Examine the evidence
Two main evidences: (1) Quranic verses implying that no person is left without guidance (75:36, 35:24); (2) Reports of the Prophet's acts of worship (fasting, prayer, hajj, tahannuth) before prophethood, which must be based on previous sharia since reason does not prescribe them.
The text says these acts provide 'necessary knowledge' (ilm daruri) that he intended obedience.
4
Compare opposing views
List the positions: Malikis and majority of Mutakallimun deny it; Mu'tazila say it is impossible; some (like al-Qadi Abu Bakr) say it did not happen; others (al-Juwayni, al-Ghazali, al-Amidi) suspend judgment.
The dispute is only in furu' (subsidiary matters); in 'aqidah there is consensus that he was bound by all previous sharias.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If he was bound, his pre-prophethood actions can be used as shar'i evidence for rulings; if not, those actions are not necessarily authoritative for us.
This practical consequence affects istinbat from his biography.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: المختار أنه صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم متعبد بشرع قبل بعثته فقيل) بشرع (آدم وقيل) بشرع (نوح وقيل) بشرع (إبراهيم وقيل بشرع (موسى وقيل) بشرع (عيسى، والأشبه ما بلغه أي تعبده بشرع بلغة من الشرائع لا بل الأشبه بشرع لم ينسخ، لكن على أنه حكم الله تعالى لا حكم ذلك النبي لأن العمل بشرع منسوخ حرام وبغير المنسوخ واجب، وهو عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام معصوم من ارتكاب الحرام وترك الواجب، ثم إنه كان نبياً وآدم بين الروح والجسد، فلا يتبع أحداً من الرسل الذين هم كالخلفاء له، فلا يتعبد إلا من جهة أنه حكم الله تعالى لا غير، ثم تعيين ذلك الشرع مما لم يقم عليه دليل فيتوقف، ويظن أنه شرع إبراهيم، فإن شريعته كانت عامة وشرع كان مختصاً بقوم فالأشبه اتباعه لشرع إبراهيم ونفاه المالكية وجمهور المتكلمين فالمعتزلة قالوا : التعبد بشرع (مستحيل) عليه صلوات الله وسلامه وآله وأصحابه عليه وأهل الحق من النافين وهم بعض أهل السنة القامعين للبدعة التعبد بشرع (غير واقع) وإن كان يجوز عقلاً وعليه القاضي أبو بكر من الشافعية وتوقف الإمام إمام الحرمين (و) الإمام حجة الإسلام الغزالي قدس سره والآمدي والنزاع) إنما هو (في الفروع، وأما العقائد فاتفاق على أنه صلوات الله وسلامه عليه وآله وأصحابه متعبد فيها بالشرائع كلها، وهو ظاهر لنا أن الناس لم يتركوا سدى من بعثة (آدم) إلى آخر الأيام، قال الله تعالى: أَيَحْسَبُ الإِنسَنُ أَن يُتْرَكَ سُدى ) [القيامة : ٣٦] وقال تعالى: ﴿وَإِن مِنْ أُمَّةٍ إِلَّا خَلَا فِيهَا نذير [فاطر: ٢٤] (فلزم التعبد لكل من بلغ إلى أن ينتسخ ويرتفع من البين لأنه حكم من تعالى متعلق بالمكلف والمعصية منفية واستدل بتضافر روایات صومه وصلاته وحجه وتحنثه) أنه كان يتحنث بغار حراء (وتلك أعمال شرعية ) البتة تفيد علماً ضرورياً أنه يقصد صح الطاعة بها فتلك الطاعات إما عرفت بالعقل أو بالشرع السابق (ولا) حكم للعقل فتعين الثاني.
Issue: The chosen view is that he, peace be upon him and his family and companions, was bound by a sharia before his mission. It is said (by the sharia of Adam, and it is said by the sharia of Noah, and it is said by the sharia of Abraham, and it is said by the sharia of Moses, and it is said by the sharia of Jesus. The most similar is that he was bound by a sharia in the language of the sharias, not that he followed a sharia that had not been abrogated, but rather as a ruling of Allah the Most High, not the ruling of that prophet. Because acting according to an abrogated sharia is forbidden, and according to a non-abrogated sharia is obligatory, and he, peace be upon him and his family and companions, is infallible from committing the forbidden and leaving the obligatory. Moreover, he was a prophet while Adam was between soul and body, so he does not follow any of the messengers who are like his deputies. He is only bound from the aspect that it is a ruling of Allah, nothing else. Then the specification of that sharia is not established by evidence, so one suspends judgment. It is thought that it is the sharia of Abraham, because his sharia was general, and other sharias were specific to their people. So it is more similar that he followed the sharia of Abraham. The Malikis and the majority of the mutakallimun denied this. The Mu'tazila said: Being bound by a sharia is impossible for him, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions. The people of truth among the deniers, who are some of the Ahl al-Sunnah who suppress innovation, said: Being bound by a sharia is not realized (i.e., did not happen), although it is intellectually possible. Upon this view are al-Qadi Abu Bakr (from the Shafi'is), and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) suspended judgment, and Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali (may his secret be sanctified) and al-Amidi. The dispute is only in subsidiary matters. As for beliefs, there is consensus that he, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions, is bound by all the sharias in them. This is apparent to us because people were not left neglected from the mission of Adam to the end of days. Allah says: 'Does man think that he will be left neglected?' (Qiyamah 75:36) and He says: 'And there is no nation but a warner has passed among them' (Fatir 35:24). So being bound by a sharia is necessary for everyone who reaches maturity until it is abrogated and removed, because it is a ruling of the Most High relating to the legally responsible person. Also sin is negated. They argued by the multiple reports of his fasting, prayer, pilgrimage, and tahannuth (devotion) in the cave of Hira, and these are certainly religious acts, providing necessary knowledge that he intended the correctness of obedience through them. Those acts of obedience were either known through reason or through previous sharia. Reason has no ruling, so the second is determined.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage addresses a fundamental usul issue: whether Prophet Muhammad (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) was bound by the sharia of any previous prophet before his own prophethood. The preferred view (mukhtar) is that he was indeed bound by some previous sharia, but not as a follower of that prophet in the ordinary sense; rather, he followed it as a ruling of Allah that continued to be applicable to him. The specification is not certain, but the most likely is the sharia of Ibrahim (alayhi al-salam), because it was a general sharia for all people. The passage notes the views of various groups: Malikis and most mutakallimun deny it; Mu'tazila say it is impossible; some say it is not realized even if possible; others suspend judgment. However, there is agreement that in matters of 'aqidah (beliefs) he was bound by all previous sharias. The evidence includes Quranic verses indicating that no person or nation is left without guidance, and the multiple reports of the Prophet's own acts of worship before prophethood, such as fasting, prayer, hajj, and tahannuth in the cave of Hira. Since these acts are clearly religious, they must have been based on previous sharia, as reason alone does not prescribe them. Therefore, this supports the view that he was bound by some previous sharia.

Key Terms
التعبد (al-ta'abbud): Being bound by a religious law (sharia); having it as a legal obligation.
الشرع السابق (al-shar' al-sabiq): Previous sharia, the law of a prior prophet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the preferred view (mukhtar) regarding the Prophet being bound by previous sharia?
A) He was not bound at all
B) He was bound by the sharia of all previous prophets
C) He was bound by some previous sharia (likely that of Ibrahim) as Allah's ruling
D) He was bound only in matters of aqidah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position affirms that the Prophet was bound by a previous sharia as a divine ruling, supported by Quranic principles and historical reports of his worship, with the sharia of Ibrahim being the most plausible.

The Authority of Companions' Statements in Hanafi Usul
Classification of Sahaba statements and their probative value.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Companions' statements and evaluate their probative value relative to authentic hadith.

Core Idea
A Companion's statement is weaker than a marfu' hadith because it may be based on opinion, even from the most reliable Companions.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut, the author addresses the probative value of the sayings of the Companions (aqwal al-sahabah). He refines a classification by Sheikh Hidad and shows why these statements cannot contradict authentic hadith, providing a foundational principle for the Hanafi method of weighing evidences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of Sahaba statements
Sheikh Hidad divided Sahaba statements into those based on opinion and those on matters not perceptible by opinion. The author further refines this.
2
Understand the division of Companions
Companions are divided into those with certain justice (like the people of Bay'at al-Ridwan) and those with merely apparent justice.
3
Analyze the probative force of each group
For the second group, their statements only give probable indication of hearing. For the first, although their justice is certain, it is still possible their fatwa was based on opinion, so their statements do not give certainty of hearing.
4
Apply the hierarchy
Since Sahaba statements are lower than authentic hadith, they cannot oppose a marfu' hadith. Only when all stronger evidences fall do they become operative.
5
Consider the practical consequence
When a Companion's statement conflicts with an authentic hadith, Hanafis prioritize the hadith, unlike some other schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
، أن مظنون أذهاننا الثالث : ما أورده الشيخ الهداد أن أقوال الصحابة قسمان : قول فيما يدرك بالرأي، وهو غير الخبر إن كان حجة فيعتبر عند تعارض الأخبار، وقول فيما لا يدرك بالرأي، وينبغي لا يعتبر عند تعارض الأخبار، لأن جهة الخبرية متعينة فهو أيضاً خبر، فليس دونه، فينبغي أن يسقط أيضاً، وأيضاً هو من نوع المتعارضين والمتكلم بهما وبه واحد، وقد جعل صاحب «الهداية» قول ابن مسعود بتغليظ الدية أرباعاً كالمرفوع فيعارض به فقد جعله كالخبر في المعارضة، وقال: مراد المشايخ بأقوال لصحابة الأقوال التي فيما يدرك بالرأي، لا كما في المستوفى من التعميم وتحقيق الحق فيه ما نقص عليك أن الصحابة منهم من هم مقطوع العدالة، كأصحاب بيعة الرضوان وبعض من تشرفوا بالصحبة بعده، ومنهم من هم العدالة، فأقول : الفريق الثاني ظاهر أنها إنما تدل على السماع ظناً لاحتمال الفتوى من غير دليل، ولو كان احتمالاً مرجوحاً فأقوالهم وإن كانت راجعة إلى الخبر لكنها دونه البتة، وأما أقوال الفريق الأول فإن كان كون فتواهم من دليل بيقين لمقطوعية عدالتهم، لكن كونها مما لا يدرك بالرأي غير مقطوع به، بل غاية الأمر الظن، وغاية العلم أنه لا يصل إليه رأينا، وأما الصحابة رضوان الله تعالى عليهم فلما كان رأيهم أعلى من رأينا وأذهانهم ثاقبة من وعقولهم متوقدة بنور إلهي احتمل أن يكون رأيهم قد وصل فأفتوا بالرأي، فههنا احتمال كون مذهبهم بالرأي قائماً فلا يدل قطعاً على السماع نعم : الظاهر السماع فيكون أدون من الخبر الصحيح، وإذا كان أدون فلا يصلح معارضاً للسنة، فيضمحل عند قيامها، وإذا تساقطا فيقوم حجة فيعمل به. وأما قول صاحب الهداية) ففي خبر خاص، ولعل فيه نوعاً من الضعف صار به مثل الخبر المظنون من فتوی صحابي بدري رضوي ذي مناقب علية منصوص عليه بالعدالة، والفضل بنص محكم الذي صح فيه مرفوعاً تمسكوا بعهد ابن أم عبد فافهم (وإلا) وجد الأدنى فالعمل الأصل) لازم، فإن العمل بالأصل عند عدم دليل أصل متأصل في الباب (كما في سؤر الحمار) فإنه نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم عن لحوم الحمر الأهلية خيبر كما في الصحيحين»، وقد عارضه قوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم للسائل عن أكل لحوم الحمر الأهلية كل من سمان أموالك) رواه البخاري والحرمة آية النجاسة، والحل آية الطهارة، فقد تعارضا، وليس ههنا أصل يقاس عليه فإن كان الهرة فالعلة فيه الضرورة الشديدة وليست مثلها في الحمار لأنها تدخل المضايق بخلافه، وإن كان السباع فليس فيها ضرورة أصلاً بخلاف الحمار، فقررنا الأصول، وهو أن الماء وجد في الأصل طاهراً، يوم
That our minds conjecture the third: what Sheikh Hidad raised that the sayings of the Companions are of two types: a statement in what is perceived by opinion, and it is other than report; if it is a proof, it is considered when reports conflict; and a statement in what is not perceived by opinion, and it should not be considered when reports conflict, because the aspect of report is determined, so it is also a report, so it is not below it, so it should also drop. Also it is of the same type as the conflicting ones, and the speaker of both is one. The author of al-Hidayah made the statement of Ibn Mas'ud about quadruple blood-money like a marfu' hadith, thereby making it like a report in opposition. He said: The intent of the mashayikh by 'sayings of the Companions' is the statements regarding what is perceived by opinion, not as in al-Mustawfa of generalization. And the investigation of the truth in it is what I relate to you: the Companions include those whose justice is certain, like the people of Bay'at al-Ridwan and some who were honored with companionship later, and among them are those whose justice is not certain. So I say: the second group apparently indicates hearing only by conjecture due to the possibility of fatwa without proof, even if it is a weak possibility. So their statements, even if they refer to a report, are definitely below it. As for the statements of the first group: although their fatwa is from proof with certainty due to the definitiveness of their justice, yet the fact that it is something not perceived by opinion is not certain; rather, the utmost is conjecture, and the extent of knowledge is that our opinion does not reach it. As for the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, since their opinion is higher than ours and their minds are sharp and their intellects enlivened by divine light, it is possible that their opinion reached and they gave fatwa by opinion. So here the possibility of their madhhab being by opinion exists, so it does not definitely indicate hearing. Yes, the apparent is hearing, so it is lower than the authentic report. And if it is lower, it is not fit to oppose the Sunnah, so it disappears when the Sunnah stands. And if they both fall, it stands as a proof and is acted upon. As for the statement of the author of al-Hidayah, it is concerning a specific report, and perhaps there is a type of weakness in it that makes it like the conjectural report from the fatwa of a Badri Companion, Rizwi, with excellent merits, specified with justice, and virtue by a definitive text that is authentically reported as marfu': 'Hold fast to the covenant of Ibn Umm Abd', so understand. (Otherwise) if the lower is found, then acting upon the original is necessary. Indeed, acting upon the original in the absence of proof is deeply rooted in the chapter. (Like in the case of the leftover water of a donkey): The Messenger of Allah, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and Companions, forbade the meat of domesticated donkeys on the day of Khaybar, as in the Sahihayn. And it was opposed by his statement to the questioner about eating meat of domesticated donkeys: 'Eat from what is in your wealth,' recorded by al-Bukhari. The prohibition is a sign of impurity, and the permission is a sign of purity, so they conflicted. And there is no original to analogize upon. For if it is the cat, the cause in it is severe necessity, and it is not like the donkey because the cat enters narrow places, unlike it. And if it is the predator, there is no necessity at all in it, unlike the donkey. So we established the original, that water is originally pure. (The text ends abruptly with 'day').
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This final passage deals with the third part of the discussion: the authority of Companions' statements (aqwal al-sahabah). Sheikh Hidad had argued that these statements are of two types: those based on opinion (which are not reports) and those based on matters not perceptible by opinion (which are reports). The author refines this classification by examining the Companions themselves. He divides them into those with definite justice (like the people of Bay'at al-Ridwan) and those with uncertain justice. For the latter, their statements only give probable indication of hearing from the Prophet, but they are still inferior to authentic hadith. For the former, though their justice is certain, it is still possible that their statements were based on their own superior reasoning, so they do not definitely prove hearing. Therefore, Companions' statements are always weaker than authentic marfu' hadith. They cannot oppose a Sunnah; if they do, the Sunnah prevails. Only when both conflicting stronger evidences fall do Companions' statements become operative. The text then gives a practical illustration: the case of su'r al-himar (the leftover water of a donkey). Two hadiths conflict: one forbids donkey meat (implying impurity) and another permits it (implying purity). Since there is no clear analogy to resolve the conflict (the cat analogy fails due to specific necessity, and predators have no necessity), the scholars fall back on the original principle: water is pure. This demonstrates the application of the rule that when strong evidences conflict and no preponderance is possible, the weaker evidences (or original principles) are acted upon.

Key Terms
قول الصحابي (qawl al-sahabi): A statement from a Companion of the Prophet, which may be based on direct hearing or personal reasoning.
بيعة الرضوان (bay'at al-ridwan): The pledge of allegiance given by the Companions under a tree at Hudaybiyyah, whose participants are guaranteed Paradise and are considered of definitive justice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position explained in the text, when a Sahaba statement conflicts with an authentic marfu' hadith, which should be preferred?
A) The Sahaba statement
B) The marfu' hadith
C) It depends on the Companion
D) They are considered equal

Key Takeaway
Companions' statements are inferior to authentic hadith and cannot oppose them.

Applying the Principle: The Case of Donkey's Leftover Water (Su'r al-Himar)
Practical application of evidence hierarchy to su'r al-himar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the usul principle of falling back to the original ruling when conflicting strong evidences cancel out, using the case of su'r al-himar.

Core Idea
When strong evidences conflict and no preponderance is possible, the original ruling (al-asl) governs.

After establishing the hierarchy of evidences, the passage applies the principle to a concrete case: the leftover water of a donkey. Two hadith conflict, and analogy fails. The scholars return to the original rule that water is pure, demonstrating a key Hanafi method for resolving apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting hadith
One forbids donkey meat (implying impurity), another permits it (implying purity).
2
Examine the attempted analogies
The text considers analogy to cat and predators but rejects both because the ruling on cat is based on necessity and predators lack necessity.
3
Recognize the failure of tarjih
Since neither hadith is preponderant and no valid analogy exists, the strong evidences fall into ta'arud (conflict).
4
Apply the fallback rule
When strong evidences fall, the original principle (al-asl) is revived: water is pure. So the leftover water is considered pure.
